<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer14555978573972960778">What on earth could have provoked a modern day St. Valentine's Day massacre?<br /><br />On Valentine's Day, four members of the Coverdale family--George, Jacqueline, Melinda and Giles--were murdered in the space of 15 minutes. Their housekeeper, Eunice Parchman, shot them, one by one, in the blue light of a televised performance of <strong>Don Giovanni</strong>. When Detective Chief Superintendent Wil</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14555978573972960778">What on earth could have provoked a modern day St. Valentine's Day massacre?<br /><br />On Valentine's Day, four members of the Coverdale family--George, Jacqueline, Melinda and Giles--were murdered in the space of 15 minutes. Their housekeeper, Eunice Parchman, shot them, one by one, in the blue light of a televised performance of <strong>Don Giovanni</strong>. When Detective Chief Superintendent William Vetch arrests Miss Parchman two weeks later, he discovers a second tragedy: the key to the Valentine's Day massacre hidden within a private humiliation Eunice Parchman has guarded all her life.  A brilliant rendering of character, motive, and the heady discovery of truth, <strong>A Judgement in Stone</strong> is among Ruth Rendell's finest psychological thrillers.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14555978573972960778">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1997292">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1997292" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin" class="left imgcol" title="Bill  Kerwin"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442940975p2/83582.jpg" alt="Bill  Kerwin" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1997292?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 04, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin" class="user" title="Bill  Kerwin">Bill  Kerwin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=detective-mystery" class="actionLinkLite">detective-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1997292">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13636048362118684728"><br />Often deemed the greatest work of one of the world's great crime novelists, <em>A Judgement in Stone</em> is justly famous for its arresting first sentence: “"Eunice Parchman killed the Coverdale family because she could not read or write." But it is not often appreciated that, however straightforward and revelatory it may appear, this sentence—and the upper-class narrator who utters it—suggests an artful and deceptive interpretation of a socially complex crime. <br /><br />Sure, Eunice kills because she is illitera</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13636048362118684728"><br />Often deemed the greatest work of one of the world's great crime novelists, <em>A Judgement in Stone</em> is justly famous for its arresting first sentence: “"Eunice Parchman killed the Coverdale family because she could not read or write." But it is not often appreciated that, however straightforward and revelatory it may appear, this sentence—and the upper-class narrator who utters it—suggests an artful and deceptive interpretation of a socially complex crime. <br /><br />Sure, Eunice kills because she is illiterate, but there are many contributing factors: suppressed lower-class rage, lower-class shame, aristocratic arrogance and paternalistic meddling, the general obliviousness of employers and their objectification of “the help,” familial reticence that conceals essential information, the fear of village gossip, the effect of religious enthusiam on individual lunacy, and—perhaps most important of all—how two people can perpetrate a hideous crime neither would be capable of completing on her own.<br /><br />This is an absorbing tale told by a distinctive voice, a cold voice which keeps us aware of the march toward inexorable death, and yet pauses on crime's road—oh so briefly!—for moments of pity and elegy. The ending, too, is a particular pleasure. I was certain the thirty pages or so after the murders would be anti-climactic and perfunctory, but they are not: they are crisp, ironic, and one of the joys of the book.<br /><br />Oh, if you like great movies: Claude Chabrol's <em>La Ceremonie</em>, starring Sandrine Bonnaire and Isabelle Huppert as the two partners in crime, is at least as good as the book. And <em>that</em> is saying something, <br /></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_292990075">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/292990075" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/8163175-karl-marx-s-t" class="left imgcol" title="Karl Marx S.T."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451115854p2/8163175.jpg" alt="Karl Marx S.T." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/292990075?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 22, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8163175-karl-marx-s-t" class="user" title="Karl Marx S.T.">Karl Marx S.T.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/8163175-karl-marx-s-t?shelf=ruth-rendell" class="actionLinkLite">ruth-rendell</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer292990075">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1298450528661881145">I discovered Rendell’s work when I was browsing The Top 100 Crime Novels of All Time. Many of her works are included on the list that I came to the realization of maybe she's that good. Since then, I became a fan of Mrs. Rendell after reading this particular title.<br /><br />The story is about a wealthy family (wealthy in the sense that they’re all educated) who hires a spinster, Eunice Parchman to serve as their housekeeper. There isn’t much problem about her at first for the family starts to like their n</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1298450528661881145">I discovered Rendell’s work when I was browsing The Top 100 Crime Novels of All Time. Many of her works are included on the list that I came to the realization of maybe she's that good. Since then, I became a fan of Mrs. Rendell after reading this particular title.<br /><br />The story is about a wealthy family (wealthy in the sense that they’re all educated) who hires a spinster, Eunice Parchman to serve as their housekeeper. There isn’t much problem about her at first for the family starts to like their new house-proud helper as the days passed. They even encourage her to take a day off from work since she does nothing but clean. Until they start to notice something different. Eunice’s secret that will drive her to kill the concerned family. The first sentences of the novel are so revealing that you wonder what would happen in the next couple of pages. I don’t want to spoil some interested reader but the story is about the effects of something important in our lives that if one’s deprived of, will make living a difficult journey. And particularly for Eunice, to resort to something drastic thus the meaning behind the title.<br /><br />Rendell is known not for whodunits but for her whydunnits. With the presence of murder, there’s always a reason. But there was more to it than that. Everybody has reasons especially when one is compelled to do something unimaginable. The novel is adapted twice for screen with English (The Housekeeper) and a French adaptation (La Cérémonie). I’ve watched both films and in my opinion, the French one's better as Mrs. Rendell also liked it. I suggest you read the book first before watching the movie, not only because it’s the original, but it’s also complete.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1298450528661881145">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5615658413203066564">Read because of the selection in the HRF Keating list of 100 best crime &amp; mystery novels. I only got 30 pages in before getting far too exasperated to keep on reading.<br /><br />It started off in a similar vein to one of those wonderful Simenon <em>roman durs</em> but quickly went downhill as I was introduced to the soon to be murdered family.<br /><br />The main characters are all incredibly awful people and whilst I am a fan of reading about offensive people these were not the type for me. Keating described them as nice</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5615658413203066564">Read because of the selection in the HRF Keating list of 100 best crime &amp; mystery novels. I only got 30 pages in before getting far too exasperated to keep on reading.<br /><br />It started off in a similar vein to one of those wonderful Simenon <em>roman durs</em> but quickly went downhill as I was introduced to the soon to be murdered family.<br /><br />The main characters are all incredibly awful people and whilst I am a fan of reading about offensive people these were not the type for me. Keating described them as nice people, as well meaning, but everything about them screams awful snob. Perhaps I am a product of a different time and a different place and a different social situation and that accounts for it but I failed to spot anything nice in Rendells caricatures.<br /><br />The first real washout from the highly thought of Keating list, I can't say I'm too disappointed based on my previous knowledge of Ruth Rendell however.</span>

            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3162627-cleo-bannister?shelf=crime" class="actionLinkLite">crime</a>,

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer972447427">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17120741157110615248">The story itself is fairly short only 220 pages long with relatively short chapters designed to keep the pages turning at a fast rate.<br /><br />Ruth Rendell starts this book with the words 'Eunice Parchman killed the Coverdale family because she could not read or write.' giving the reader the murderer, the victims and the motive straight away, but this book kept me gripped as the story of Eunices early life, her work for the Coverdales and her friendship with Joan Smith, a zealot and wife of the village p</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17120741157110615248">The story itself is fairly short only 220 pages long with relatively short chapters designed to keep the pages turning at a fast rate.<br /><br />Ruth Rendell starts this book with the words 'Eunice Parchman killed the Coverdale family because she could not read or write.' giving the reader the murderer, the victims and the motive straight away, but this book kept me gripped as the story of Eunices early life, her work for the Coverdales and her friendship with Joan Smith, a zealot and wife of the village postmaster.<br /><br />Ruth Rendell gives us sharp observations about not only Eunice but the Coverdale family headed by George, a pompous managing director and his younger wife Jaqueline, who prefers meeting with friends and having her hair done, to keeping on top of the cleaning at the manor house they lived in Lowfield Hall.<br /><br />This is a stand alone story, not one of the Wexford mysteries and show cases her psychologically based stories very convincingly. The setting of a small village in England in the early 70's fuelled by gossip in the local shop and the pub, The Blue Boar, all add to the bewilderment when the family is slayed.</span>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_922519952">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/922519952" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2953336-holly" class="left imgcol" title="Holly"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1258493037p2/2953336.jpg" alt="Holly" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/922519952?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 03, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2953336-holly" class="user" title="Holly">Holly</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer922519952">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13931229794422594529">Count me amongst those who are not fans of this book. Ok, the characters were well drawn. Neither the characters nor the story were unbelievable. So, I guess that's good. And it was well written, in the sense that there was nothing trite or annoying.<br /><br />But I didn't enjoy reading this book at all. I would actually go as far to say that it was an unpleasant experience. From the beginning, the reader is told the whole story: that Eunice kills the Cloverdale family because she is illiterate. I suppose</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13931229794422594529">Count me amongst those who are not fans of this book. Ok, the characters were well drawn. Neither the characters nor the story were unbelievable. So, I guess that's good. And it was well written, in the sense that there was nothing trite or annoying.<br /><br />But I didn't enjoy reading this book at all. I would actually go as far to say that it was an unpleasant experience. From the beginning, the reader is told the whole story: that Eunice kills the Cloverdale family because she is illiterate. I suppose the suspense of the book plays out in terms of how such a minor issue could lead to murder. But, knowing this end just leaves the reader to dread what is coming--the death of a family who fundamentally doesn't deserve it and, while somewhat shallow, are people with good intentions.<br /><br />The only suspense in the end came from the dramatic irony of knowing what was coming, and I didn't like that, as I said above. The only interesting part of the story to me was the last 30 pages, once the inspectors from the police showed up and were trying to figure out what had happened.<br /><br />On the cover, there are raves that Ruth Rendell couldn't write a better novel. I know she has written many more, so I hope this isn't true. The book was written in 1977, so I wondered as I read if, in the days before the true crime books and shows like 48 Hours Mystery, which outline all kinds of real life cases with bizarre circumstances and motives, this book might have been more appealing to the reader. At any rate, I wouldn't recommend it to anyone. I think I will give some of Ruth Rendell's other works a try--but those that concern police investigations as opposed to another like this one.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13931229794422594529">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_284554658">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/284554658" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1883816-barbara" class="left imgcol" title="Barbara"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1448379218p2/1883816.jpg" alt="Barbara" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/284554658?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 12, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1883816-barbara" class="user" title="Barbara">Barbara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1883816-barbara?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1883816-barbara?shelf=suspense" class="actionLinkLite">suspense</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer284554658">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7081959270529755828">This is one of Rendell's most amazing, suspenseful books!<br /><br /><br /><br />We members of Goodreads could scarcely imagine what it would be like to be unable to read. Much has been written about illiterate adults. We are aware of the efforts these people go to in order to conceal this deficit from those around them. Many have developed coping strategies or employ artifice. This book, written earlier in Ruth Rendell’s career, has deftly described such an individual, Eunice Parchman. It is especially interesting to</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7081959270529755828">This is one of Rendell's most amazing, suspenseful books!<br /><br /><br /><br />We members of Goodreads could scarcely imagine what it would be like to be unable to read. Much has been written about illiterate adults. We are aware of the efforts these people go to in order to conceal this deficit from those around them. Many have developed coping strategies or employ artifice. This book, written earlier in Ruth Rendell’s career, has deftly described such an individual, Eunice Parchman. It is especially interesting to note that the behaviors of this woman surround her fears of detection, causing her to erect an icy, impenetrable barrier from others.<br /><br /> Rendell’s chilling plot, written with her elegant prose, builds in intensity, gripping the attention of the reader. Her main character and her closest associate act in ways that would strain ordinary belief. In viewing these arrogant and self-serving exploits,one can begin to analyze and comprehend their roots as this author has so brilliantly rendered them in her narrative.<br /><br />I continue to feel awe in Rendell’s ability to devise such pathological human nature, to delve so deeply in her character developments and to demonstrate such insight and tension in her plots.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7081959270529755828">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12363726169562232765">This is well written but a bit heavy handed. I think it's interesting that an illiterate woman WHO IS ALSO ALREADY A MURDERER, can find solace with a nutjob fundamentalist who dresses like a whore and can conspire with her to commit murder, but I think the emphasis was too much on Eunice's illiteracy. The result is some of the reviews here actually say that the book shows how illiteracy can lead to murder. Actually, the book shows how having a shameful secret can lead a psychopath to murder (aga</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12363726169562232765">This is well written but a bit heavy handed. I think it's interesting that an illiterate woman WHO IS ALSO ALREADY A MURDERER, can find solace with a nutjob fundamentalist who dresses like a whore and can conspire with her to commit murder, but I think the emphasis was too much on Eunice's illiteracy. The result is some of the reviews here actually say that the book shows how illiteracy can lead to murder. Actually, the book shows how having a shameful secret can lead a psychopath to murder (again) with the help of a mentally ill but "helpful" friend. It's more of a Dick and Perry story than anything about illiteracy. FWIW, the only illiterate person I knew was a classmate's neighbor in high school. She was not shy and dishonest, she was a heartbreakingly underconfident but still reasonably well adjusted teenaged girl. She probably had some undiagnosed problem, and I hope she eventually found help.<br /><br />I found the structure unappealing. I didn't want to read any more about the Coverdales and what led up to their murders. I read to about page 90 then skipped to the end. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer465540671933044370">One Sunday evening on Valentine's Day, Eunice Parchmand and Joan Smith shotgun to death four members of the Coverdale family as they watch an opera on television. Ruth Rendell delves deep into the psychological nuances of the two cracked killers, and Rendell demonstrates that one illiterate character who interacts with one psychotic character can only end in a disaster. When Rendell sets up this thesis, then the rest of the novel becomes, more or less, "A Count Down to Mass Murder". This book is</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText465540671933044370">One Sunday evening on Valentine's Day, Eunice Parchmand and Joan Smith shotgun to death four members of the Coverdale family as they watch an opera on television. Ruth Rendell delves deep into the psychological nuances of the two cracked killers, and Rendell demonstrates that one illiterate character who interacts with one psychotic character can only end in a disaster. When Rendell sets up this thesis, then the rest of the novel becomes, more or less, "A Count Down to Mass Murder". This book is the basis for Claude Chabrol's excellent film noir, "La Ceremonie". Chabrol treats the material as a Murder Mystery, whereas Rendell informs the reader about the killings in the first paragraph. A compelling set of circumstances makes for an excellent read, and I can't help but wonder how Patricia Highsmith would have handled this collection of interesting characters and bizarre plot? </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11525591142650547289">In Berhard Schlink's The Reader, we learn that former Nazi guard Hanna Schmitz is prepared to accept the responsibility of mass murder and a consequent lengthy prison sentence rather than admit to the shame of illiteracy. She abandons everthing to keep this secret. Rendell's chilling novel, on a smaller human scale, has similar elements: Eunice Parchman's is illiterate, secretive, and deadly. She is also, however, a psychopath (with an extremely unappealing persona) and an unquestionable misanth</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11525591142650547289">In Berhard Schlink's The Reader, we learn that former Nazi guard Hanna Schmitz is prepared to accept the responsibility of mass murder and a consequent lengthy prison sentence rather than admit to the shame of illiteracy. She abandons everthing to keep this secret. Rendell's chilling novel, on a smaller human scale, has similar elements: Eunice Parchman's is illiterate, secretive, and deadly. She is also, however, a psychopath (with an extremely unappealing persona) and an unquestionable misanthrope. The results are as deadly as Hanna's although the numbers are smaller and the reasons less profound. The writing is succinct and the tension is taunt. A fine page turner that became an excellent film, Le Ceremonie, in 1995 by Claude Chabrol. </span>

A plot thread, told in the first sentence, was the murderess couldn't read or write, something she attempted to keep secret. Rendall can create a plot, characters and deliver a book that is similar to P. D. James. (And I seem to have read that they are friends.) This is a satisfying novel--I can't really say it fits the word mystery, though that it where it will be found on most library shelves.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_961566821">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/961566821" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4806357-bev" class="left imgcol" title="Bev"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1333510938p2/4806357.jpg" alt="Bev" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/961566821?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 08, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4806357-bev" class="user" title="Bev">Bev</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4806357-bev?shelf=crime-mystery" class="actionLinkLite">crime-mystery</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4806357-bev?shelf=logos" class="actionLinkLite">logos</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer961566821">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2826297355421917416">In a small town in England, "The St. Valentine's Day Massacre" has nothing to do with roaring twenties US gangsters. It was on February 14 that housekeeper Eunice Parchman killed four members of the Coverdale family, for whom she worked, while they sat in their living room watching a performance of Don Giovanni on television. The murder is announced at the start of the book, the build-up to why the family were murdered and how the crime was solved is the substance of this book. The direct reason</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2826297355421917416">In a small town in England, "The St. Valentine's Day Massacre" has nothing to do with roaring twenties US gangsters. It was on February 14 that housekeeper Eunice Parchman killed four members of the Coverdale family, for whom she worked, while they sat in their living room watching a performance of Don Giovanni on television. The murder is announced at the start of the book, the build-up to why the family were murdered and how the crime was solved is the substance of this book. The direct reason for Eunice's actions is rooted in her most deeply held secret -- she has never learned to read. She copes well, but misunderstandings and misinterpretations are inevitable when one is ashamed to have that fact known. It was the reason she killed the family...and the reason she was caught by the police. In the end, her punishment for this crime was exacerbated by the fact that everyone learned her life-long held secret. While there is little "suspense" in this book, it is more a psychological study of a sociopath and how she blends in with the life around her.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2826297355421917416">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span id="like_container_review_961566821" class="likeItContainer"><a href="/rating/voters/961566821?resource_type=Review" rel="nofollow" id="like_count_review_961566821"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a> · <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F83412.A_Judgement_in_Stone" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" rel="nofollow">Like</a></span> · <a href="/review/show/961566821?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1">see review</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1296362694">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1296362694" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6098374-roger-pettit" class="left imgcol" title="Roger Pettit"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Roger Pettit" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1296362694?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 02, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6098374-roger-pettit" class="user" title="Roger Pettit">Roger Pettit</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1296362694">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15162915742012599190">Ruth Rendell, who died earlier this year, is widely regarded as one of the finest crime novelists of recent times. I understand that her final book, which she completed before her death, will be published this autumn. I have read a fair amount of her work and enjoyed most of it. I was slightly disappointed, however, with two of her most recent books - 'Tigerlily's Orchids' and 'The St Zita Society'. So I thought I'd try 'A Judgement in Stone', one of her early standalone psychological thrillers,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15162915742012599190">Ruth Rendell, who died earlier this year, is widely regarded as one of the finest crime novelists of recent times. I understand that her final book, which she completed before her death, will be published this autumn. I have read a fair amount of her work and enjoyed most of it. I was slightly disappointed, however, with two of her most recent books - 'Tigerlily's Orchids' and 'The St Zita Society'. So I thought I'd try 'A Judgement in Stone', one of her early standalone psychological thrillers, in the hope that it would remind me just how good a writer she can be. And I am so pleased that I did. It's a very good novel, one of her best. <br /><br />It's clear from the outset of 'A Judgement in Stone' exactly what crime has been committed and who the victims and the perpetrators are. So, there is no puzzle element, as there is in many of Rendell's books that feature her popular series character Detective Chief Inspector Reg Wexford. Instead, what we have is a fascinating study in the psychology of murder and a convincing and compelling examination of isolation and of some of the murkier aspects of the human psyche. <br /><br />The story is set in Suffolk and concerns the killing of four members of the wealthy, middle-class Coverdale family by their housekeeper, Eunice Parchman, and her friend Joan Smith. The book has one of the most memorable and intriguing opening sentences of any novel I have read: 'Eunice Parchman killed the Coverdale family because she could not read or write'. Well, after those words you just have to read on! And when you do, you soon begin to realise that a writer of Rendell's skill and talent is able to fashion a very effective, gripping and entertaining crime story in which there is no mystery or whodunit element. What keeps the reader turning the pages is an eagerness to know how and why the crime in question took place. <br /><br />The characterisation in 'A Judgment in Stone' is exceptionally well done. The portrayal of Eunice, a typical loner who is cold and calculating but also weak and vulnerable, is brilliant. Despite the fact that we are aware that she is about to commit a truly wicked act, we have some sympathy for her plight and for her alienation from normal society. Her accomplice, Joan Smith, is a fascinatingly deluded and evil character. At one point, Rendell writes of her: 'One wonders what Joan Smith would have done with children if she had had them. Eaten them, perhaps.' That is spine-tinglingly excruciating and witty! It's the sort of sentence I would expect to find in one of Stanley Ellin's superbly mordant short crime stories. 'A Judgment in Stone' is also a very good critique of the British class system. It's beautifully written and splendidly paced. The gradual build-up of tension keeps the reader hooked right up to the dramatic and horrifying conclusion of the story. <br /><br />Devotees of crime fiction have much to thank Ruth Rendell for. She raised the writing of such stories to new heights in terms of quality and variety. In particular, she successfully moved things on from the often very entertaining but generally formulaic classical detective stories that had hitherto formed the bulk of published British crime fiction. 'A Judgement in Stone' is a highly entertaining and readable novel of considerable psychological depth and insight. Like much of its prolific author's other work, it's well worth a few hours of your time. 9/10.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15162915742012599190">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1444451486">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1444451486" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/47632791-sue-repasky" class="left imgcol" title="Sue Repasky"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" alt="Sue Repasky" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1444451486?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 16, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/47632791-sue-repasky" class="user" title="Sue Repasky">Sue Repasky</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/8260286-a-judgement-in-stone" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1444451486">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13358224136551470697"><strong>Early Rendell</strong><br /><br />This is early Rendell, and it shows in the clumsy form in which her narrative is set out. It is written like a magazine article about a true crime and lacks the subtlety of her later novels. Still, it hints at the genius to come as her career progressed, especially her uncanny ability to delve into the minds of the criminally insane (or in the case of many of her characters, the criminally sane, who somehow find themselves caught up in an inescapable web of murder and mayhem). Despi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13358224136551470697"><strong>Early Rendell</strong><br /><br />This is early Rendell, and it shows in the clumsy form in which her narrative is set out. It is written like a magazine article about a true crime and lacks the subtlety of her later novels. Still, it hints at the genius to come as her career progressed, especially her uncanny ability to delve into the minds of the criminally insane (or in the case of many of her characters, the criminally sane, who somehow find themselves caught up in an inescapable web of murder and mayhem). Despite the faults in this book, though, it is still a jolly good read.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13358224136551470697">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_480326914">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/480326914" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6647268-mike-gabor" class="left imgcol" title="Mike Gabor"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1384794154p2/6647268.jpg" alt="Mike Gabor" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/480326914?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 23, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6647268-mike-gabor" class="user" title="Mike Gabor">Mike Gabor</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6647268-mike-gabor?shelf=british-mystery" class="actionLinkLite">british-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer480326914">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5898733736131347377">On Valentine's Day, four members of the Coverdale family--George, Jacqueline, Melinda and Giles--were murdered in the space of 15 minutes. Their housekeeper, Eunice Parchman, shot them, one by one, in the blue light of a televised performance of Don Giovanni. When Detective Chief Superintendent William Vetch arrests Miss Parchman two weeks later, he discovers a second tragedy: the key to the Valentine's Day massacre hidden within a private humiliation Eunice Parchman has guarded all her life. <br /><br />No</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5898733736131347377">On Valentine's Day, four members of the Coverdale family--George, Jacqueline, Melinda and Giles--were murdered in the space of 15 minutes. Their housekeeper, Eunice Parchman, shot them, one by one, in the blue light of a televised performance of Don Giovanni. When Detective Chief Superintendent William Vetch arrests Miss Parchman two weeks later, he discovers a second tragedy: the key to the Valentine's Day massacre hidden within a private humiliation Eunice Parchman has guarded all her life. <br /><br />Now, this is what good writing is all about. We know right away how the ending will turn out but Rendell none the less still keeps us turning the pages. You get a wonderful sense of the Coverdale family and the how and why of their bringing their murderer into their home. You get the full picture of Eunice Parchman, and what drove her to commit this act. If you have never read a Rendell novel I suggest you start with this one.</span>

          </span>

  <a href="/user/show/16830599-rachel" class="left imgcol" title="Rachel"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1365659231p2/16830599.jpg" alt="Rachel" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <a href="/user/show/16830599-rachel" class="user" title="Rachel">Rachel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer953111483">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2207189743302213388">Such a tightly-written detective novel. Even though you know who the murderer is on the first page and why they have committed the murder, you need to know the hows and the underlying whys and the answer to the question of "What factors conspired to have this event occur?" The psychology of the novel is very interesting, if perhaps a little dated now, as the novel was written almost 40 years ago. I think what I find most fascinating about this book is that it shows the reader how much we depend</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2207189743302213388">Such a tightly-written detective novel. Even though you know who the murderer is on the first page and why they have committed the murder, you need to know the hows and the underlying whys and the answer to the question of "What factors conspired to have this event occur?" The psychology of the novel is very interesting, if perhaps a little dated now, as the novel was written almost 40 years ago. I think what I find most fascinating about this book is that it shows the reader how much we depend on literacy and how easily we assume that people can read and write. And the consequences illiteracy can have.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1357863588">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1357863588" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5981899-yvonne" class="left imgcol" title="Yvonne"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1313943444p2/5981899.jpg" alt="Yvonne" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1357863588?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 07, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5981899-yvonne" class="user" title="Yvonne">Yvonne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1357863588">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1756016293519086442">OMG, how did it take me so long to read Ruth Rendell? A Judgement in Stone is a great psychological thriller, unputdownable. The reader knows at the outset who was killed and who the murderers are. <br />But the question 'why' is what the reader needs to know the answer to. And so this strange and fascinating tale gets underway, moving ever closer to the events, as yet unknown, that result in the murder of four people. <br /><br />Eunice Parchman can neither read nor write, and has hidden it from the world. Eunic</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1756016293519086442">OMG, how did it take me so long to read Ruth Rendell? A Judgement in Stone is a great psychological thriller, unputdownable. The reader knows at the outset who was killed and who the murderers are. <br />But the question 'why' is what the reader needs to know the answer to. And so this strange and fascinating tale gets underway, moving ever closer to the events, as yet unknown, that result in the murder of four people. <br /><br />Eunice Parchman can neither read nor write, and has hidden it from the world. Eunice feels incredibly inferior as a result and is a profoundly dull person of which she has no awareness. She is a lump of a person except for her 'passion' for cleaning, which is really just an energy release. Eunice hates the written and the printed word, and so hates books, newspapers, signs, notes, magazines and so on. They represent a world she can't enter and a world she despises. Her greatest fear of all is to be found out, to be proven inferior and lacking in what everyone else takes for granted, and she goes to great lengths to prevent that discovery. <br /><br />Eunice Parchman, such a perfect name. Such a colourless, bland, unnoticeable name. 'Parchment Face' her murdered employers called her in private, but not meanly, not at first anyway. Eunice can take her place amongst female sociopath killers. Even though her crime wasn't planned, in a sense it was a long time coming and it also wasn't her first murder.<br /><br />Through a bit of encouragement from a woman Eunice knew in London, (who had her own reasons for suggesting Eunice try and better herself somewhere else), and through a misinterpretation of Eunice's character during the interview, Eunice lands a position as a live-in cleaning woman/servant at Lowfield Hall. Lowfield is a manor house located near a tiny English village and the wife of the owner, Jacqueline Cloverdale, is desperate for a house cleaner. Eunice is as surprised as her 'friend' that she gets the job and her 'friend' is glad to see her go.<br /><br />The owners of Lowfield are very similar in my mind to Lord and Lady Grantham on TV's Downton Abbey. Judgement in Stone is set in the 1970's, and the book was first published in 1977, so well before Downton. But everytime I read a line uttered by George Cloverdale, Eunice's employer, I could hear Lord Grantham's voice. But I digress.<br /><br />Eunice is an uninformed, humourless, dull, without a personality woman of no character or experience to speak of in life. Eunice basically acts on instinct. She is however a tremendous cleaner, one of few endeavours from which she finds great personal satisfaction, the other might be knitting and/or eating chocolate. She is largely a creature of no curiosity, no empathy and would prefer to be left totally alone (lest someone find out she can't read). <br /><br />Would Eunice have acted alone to kill her employer and his family? Seems unlikely, but paired with the religious lunatic Joan Smith,<br />whom she meets in the village, and becomes to all appearances friends with, events begin to spiral. Joan is a shrill harpy, skinny, loud and oblivious to anything other than what she wants or what she perceives God wants.<br /><br />A gruesome twosome, from which something wicked comes this way. This is a well written book, perhaps a classic in its genre and I don't know it. I will certainly be reading more of Miss Rendall, who also writes as Barbara Vine.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1756016293519086442">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1346762460">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1346762460" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/19647364-usfromdk" class="left imgcol" title="Usfromdk"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1379493359p2/19647364.jpg" alt="Usfromdk" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1346762460?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 27, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/19647364-usfromdk" class="user" title="Usfromdk">Usfromdk</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1346762460">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2340878315835669330">Quite poor, but I did read it to the end.<br /><br />The author seemingly aspires to give you a semi-deep look into the mind of a killer, but the problem is that her killer's behaviour simply did not touch me as remotely realistic. The book also doesn't work for me on account of the implausibility of the setting; it's not remotely plausible that a secretly illiterate house-keeper would ever be able to live in a household like that and keep her inability to read secret for any length of time, and I'm tempted</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2340878315835669330">Quite poor, but I did read it to the end.<br /><br />The author seemingly aspires to give you a semi-deep look into the mind of a killer, but the problem is that her killer's behaviour simply did not touch me as remotely realistic. The book also doesn't work for me on account of the implausibility of the setting; it's not remotely plausible that a secretly illiterate house-keeper would ever be able to live in a household like that and keep her inability to read secret for any length of time, and I'm tempted to say that anyone who'd assume otherwise don't know any people like the Coverdales. The occasional threats of discovery included in the book would in a remotely realistic setting be daily (hourly?) occurrences in a household like that, and she'd been 'found out' very fast. A related irritating aspect is that Rendell seems to want to have it both ways; it's incredibly difficult to avoid touching upon stuff from books or other written materials in everyday conversations, she claims multiple times, yet the main character seems to manage just fine for months in a household where this should be practically impossible, considering the educational level and sophistication/social class of the other characters. It doesn't fit, it doesn't work.<br /><br />To me the premise simply fell flat from the outset. No, she did not become a murderess because she couldn't read. I still have no clue why she did murder those people, because the authors' 'explanation', to the extent that one is given, is stupid. If this had actually happened it'd be an interesting abnormal psychology read - but unfortunately it's all made up.<br /><br />If this is supposed to be one of Rendell's best books, I'm hesitant to consider reading any more of her books.</span>

It will take an amateur to—inadvertently—show them the way.\n \nCheerful, charming, and always eager for a mystery, portrait artist and gentleman sleuth Philip Trent leaps into the Manderson affair with all the passion of the autodidact. Simply by reading the newspapers, he discovers overlooked details of the crime. Not all of his reasoning is sound, and his romantic interests are suspect, to say the least, but Trent’s dedication to the art of detection soon uncovers what no one expected him to find: the truth.\n     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/10890.Ruth_Rendell">About Ruth Rendell</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/10890.Ruth_Rendell"><img alt="10890" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1196257541p2/10890.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer12622329060324307835">A.K.A. <a title="Barbara Vine" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/47687.Barbara_Vine">Barbara Vine</a><br /><br />Ruth Barbara Rendell, Baroness Rendell of Babergh, CBE, who also wrote under the pseudonym <a title="Barbara Vine" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/47687.Barbara_Vine">Barbara Vine</a>, was an acclaimed English crime writer, known for her many psychological thrillers and murder mysteries and above all for Inspector Wexford.</span>

    </div>
